Another, separate, form of confirmation bias also plays a role, where believers often fail to distinguish between messages that demonstrate special ability and those that do not.<sup id="cite_ref-Raymond_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raymond-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:180â€“181</sup> Thus there are two distinct forms of confirmation bias that are under study with respect to astrological belief.<sup id="cite_ref-Raymond_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raymond-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:180â€“181</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demarcation">Demarcation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Astrology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Demarcation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under the criterion of <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsifiability</a>, first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Philosopher_of_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosopher of science">philosopher of science</a> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>, astrology is a pseudoscience.<sup id="cite_ref-PopperStanford_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PopperStanford-119">[118]</a></sup> Popper regarded astrology as "pseudo-empirical" in that "it appeals to observation and experiment," but "nevertheless does not come up to scientific standards."<sup id="cite_ref-Popper_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Popper-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:44</sup> In contrast to scientific disciplines, astrology has not responded to falsification through experiment.<sup id="cite_ref-Cogan_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cogan-121">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:206</sup></p>
<p>In contrast to Popper, the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kuhn" title="Thomas Kuhn">Thomas Kuhn</a> argued that it was not lack of falsifiability that makes astrology unscientific, but rather that the process and concepts of astrology are non-empirical.<sup id="cite_ref-Wright_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:401</sup> Kuhn thought that, though astrologers had, historically, made predictions that categorically failed, this in itself does not make it unscientific, nor do attempts by astrologers to explain away failures by claiming that creating a horoscope is very difficult. Rather, in Kuhn's eyes, astrology is not science because it was always more akin to <a href="/wiki/Medieval_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval medicine">medieval medicine</a>; they followed a sequence of rules and guidelines for a seemingly necessary field with known shortcomings, but they did no research because the fields are not amenable to research,<sup id="cite_ref-Kuhn_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhn-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> and so "they had no puzzles to solve and therefore no science to practise."<sup id="cite_ref-Wright_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:401;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kuhn_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhn-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> While an astronomer could correct for failure, an astrologer could not. An astrologer could only explain away failure but could not revise the astrological <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> in a meaningful way. As such, to Kuhn, even if the stars could influence the path of humans through life astrology is not scientific.<sup id="cite_ref-Kuhn_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhn-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup></p>
<p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Paul_Thagard" title="Paul Thagard">Paul Thagard</a> asserts that astrology cannot be regarded as falsified in this sense until it has been replaced with a successor. In the case of predicting behaviour, psychology is the alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-Thagard_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thagard-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:228</sup> To Thagard a further criterion of demarcation of science from pseudoscience is that the state-of-the-art must progress and that the community of researchers should be attempting to compare the current theory to alternatives, and not be "selective in considering confirmations and disconfirmations."<sup id="cite_ref-Thagard_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thagard-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:227â€“228</sup> Progress is defined here as explaining new phenomena and solving existing problems, yet astrology has failed to progress having only changed little in nearly 2000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Thagard_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thagard-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:228</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hurley_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurley-124">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:549</sup> To Thagard, astrologers are acting as though engaged in <a href="/wiki/Normal_science" title="Normal science">normal science</a> believing that the foundations of astrology were well established despite the "many unsolved problems," and in the face of better alternative theories (psychology). For these reasons Thagard views astrology as pseudoscience.<sup id="cite_ref-Thagard_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thagard-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hurley_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurley-124">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:228</sup></p>
<p>For the philosopher Edward W. James, astrology is irrational not because of the numerous problems with mechanisms and falsification due to experiments, but because an analysis of the astrological literature shows that it is infused with fallacious logic and poor reasoning.<sup id="cite_ref-EdwardJ_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EdwardJ-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:34</sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What if throughout astrological writings we meet little appreciation of coherence, blatant insensitivity to evidence, no sense of a hierarchy of reasons, slight command over the contextual force of critieria, stubborn unwillingless to pursue an argument where it leads, stark naivete concerning the effiacacy of explanation and so on? In that case, I think, we are perfectly justified in rejecting astrology as irrational.&#160;... Astrology simply fails to meet the multifarious demands of legitimate reasoning."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>â€”â€‰Edward W. James<sup id="cite_ref-EdwardJ_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EdwardJ-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:34</sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effectiveness">Effectiveness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Astrology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Effectiveness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Astrology has not demonstrated its effectiveness in <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">controlled studies</a> and has no scientific validity.<sup id="cite_ref-Cosmic_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmic-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:85;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zarka_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zarka-11">[11]</a></sup> Where it has made <a href="/wiki/Falsifiable" class="mw-redirect" title="Falsifiable">falsifiable</a> predictions under <a href="/wiki/Scientific_control" title="Scientific control">controlled conditions</a>, they have been falsified.<sup id="cite_ref-Zarka_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zarka-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:424</sup> One famous experiment included 28 astrologers who were asked to match over a hundred natal charts to psychological profiles generated by the <a href="/wiki/California_Psychological_Inventory" title="California Psychological Inventory">California Psychological Inventory</a> (CPI) questionnaire.<sup id="cite_ref-Muller_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muller-126">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CritThink_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CritThink-127">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Blind_experiment#Double-blind_trials" title="Blind experiment">double-blind</a> experimental protocol used in this study was agreed upon by a group of physicists and a group of astrologers<sup id="cite_ref-Zarka_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zarka-11">[11]</a></sup> nominated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_for_Geocosmic_Research" title="National Council for Geocosmic Research">National Council for Geocosmic Research</a>, who advised the experimenters, helped ensure that the test was fair<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:420;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-CritThink_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CritThink-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:117</sup> and helped draw the central proposition of <a href="/wiki/Natal_astrology" title="Natal astrology">natal astrology</a> to be tested.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:419</sup> They also chose 26 out of the 28 astrologers for the tests (two more volunteered afterwards).<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:420</sup> The study, published in <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)"><i>Nature</i></a> in 1985, found that predictions based on natal astrology were no better than chance, and that the testing "...clearly refutes the astrological hypothesis."<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1955, astrologer and psychologist Michel Gauquelin stated that though he had failed to find evidence that supported indicators like <a href="/wiki/Astrological_signs" class="mw-redirect" title="Astrological signs">zodiacal signs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astrological_aspects" class="mw-redirect" title="Astrological aspects">planetary aspects</a> in astrology, he did find positive correlations between the <a href="/wiki/Diurnal_motion" title="Diurnal motion">diurnal positions</a> of some <a href="/wiki/Planets_in_astrology" title="Planets in astrology">planets</a> and success in professions that astrology traditionally associates with those planets.<sup id="cite_ref-Pont_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pont-128">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gauquelin-1955_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gauquelin-1955-129">[128]</a></sup> The best-known of Gauquelin's findings is based on the positions of Mars in the <a href="/wiki/Natal_chart" title="Natal chart">natal charts</a> of successful athletes and became known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_effect" title="Mars effect">Mars effect</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carroll_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:213</sup> A study conducted by seven French scientists attempted to replicate the claim, but found no statistical evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-Carroll_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:213â€“214</sup> They attributed the effect to selective bias on Gauquelin's part, accusing him of attempting to persuade them to add or delete names from their study.<sup id="cite_ref-Benski_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benski-131">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geoffrey Dean has suggested that the effect may be caused by self-reporting of birth dates by parents rather than any issue with the study by Gauquelin. The suggestion is that a small subset of the parents may have had changed birth times to be consistent with better astrological charts for a related profession. The sample group was taken from a time where belief in astrology was more common. Gauquelin had failed to find the Mars effect in more recent populations, where a nurse or doctor recorded the birth information. The number of births under astrologically undesirable conditions was also lower, indicating more evidence that parents choose dates and times to suit their beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-CritThink_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CritThink-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:116</sup></p>
<p>Dean, a scientist and former astrologer, and psychologist Ivan Kelly conducted a large scale scientific test that involved more than one hundred <a href="/wiki/Cognitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Behavioural" class="mw-redirect" title="Behavioural">behavioural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiological" class="mw-redirect" title="Physiological">physical</a>, and other variablesâ€”but found no support for astrology.<sup id="cite_ref-FailToPredict_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FailToPredict-132">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey-133">[132]</a></sup> Furthermore, a <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> pooled 40 studies that involved 700 astrologers and over 1,000 birth charts. Ten of the testsâ€”which involved 300 participantsâ€”had the astrologers pick the correct chart interpretation out of a number of others that were not the astrologically correct chart interpretation (usually three to five others). When date and other obvious clues were removed, no significant results suggested there was any preferred chart.<sup id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:190</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lack_of_mechanisms_and_consistency">Lack of mechanisms and consistency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Astrology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Lack of mechanisms and consistency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Testing the validity of astrology can be difficult, because there is no consensus amongst astrologers as to what astrology is or what it can predict.<sup id="cite_ref-Cosmic_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmic-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:83</sup> Most professional astrologers are paid to predict the future or describe a person's personality and life, but most horoscopes only make vague untestable statements that can apply to almost anyone.<sup id="cite_ref-Cosmic_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmic-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Eysenck1982_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck1982-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:83</sup></p>
<p>Many astrologers claim that astrology is scientific,<sup id="cite_ref-ChrisFrench_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFrench-134">[133]</a></sup> while some have proposed conventional <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causal agents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ChrisFrench_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFrench-134">[133]</a></sup> Scientists reject these mechanisms as implausible<sup id="cite_ref-ChrisFrench_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisFrench-134">[133]</a></sup> since, for example, the magnetic field, when measured from earth, of a large but distant planet such as Jupiter is far smaller than that produced by ordinary household appliances.<sup id="cite_ref-Shermer_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shermer-135">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Western astrology has taken the earth's <a href="/wiki/Precession#Axial_precession_.28precession_of_the_equinoxes.29" title="Precession">axial precession (also called precession of the equinoxes)</a> into account since Ptolemy's <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>, so the 'first point of Aries', the start of the astrological year, continually moves against the background of the stars.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> The tropical zodiac has no connection to the stars, and as long as no claims are made that the constellations themselves are in the associated <a href="/wiki/Astrological_sign" title="Astrological sign">sign</a>, astrologers avoid the concept that precession seemingly moves the constellations.<sup id="cite_ref-Charpak_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charpak-137">[136]</a></sup> Charpak and Broch, noting this, referred to astrology based on the tropical zodiac as being "...empty boxes that have nothing to do with anything and are devoid of any consistency or correspondence with the stars."<sup id="cite_ref-Charpak_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charpak-137">[136]</a></sup> Sole use of the tropical zodiac is inconsistent with references made, by the same astrologers, to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Aquarius" title="Age of Aquarius">Age of Aquarius</a>, which depends on when the vernal point enters the constellation of Aquarius.<sup id="cite_ref-Zarka_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zarka-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Astrologers usually have only a small knowledge of astronomy, and often do not take into account basic principlesâ€”such as the precession of the equinoxes, which changes the position of the sun with time. They commented on the example of <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lizabeth_Teissier" title="Ã‰lizabeth Teissier">Ã‰lizabeth Teissier</a>, who claimed that, "The sun ends up in the same place in the sky on the same date each year," as the basis for claims that two people with the same birthday, but a number of years apart, should be under the same planetary influence. Charpak and Broch noted that, "There is a difference of about twenty-two thousand miles between Earth's location on any specific date in two successive years," and that thus they should not be under the same influence according to astrology. Over a 40 years period there would be a difference greater than 780,000 miles.<sup id="cite_ref-Charpak_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charpak-137">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Astrology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_politics_and_society">Western politics and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Astrology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Western politics and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the West, political leaders have sometimes consulted astrologers. <a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Wohl" title="Louis de Wohl">Louis de Wohl</a> worked as an astrologer for the British intelligence agency <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">MI5</a>, after claims surfaced that <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> used astrology to time his actions. The War Office was "...interested to know what Hitler's own astrologers would be telling him from week to week."<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> In fact, de Wohl's predictions were so inaccurate that he was soon labelled a "complete charlatan," and later evidence showed that Hitler considered astrology "complete nonsense."<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/John_Hinckley" class="mw-redirect" title="John Hinckley">John Hinckley's</a> <a href="/wiki/Attempted_assassination_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Attempted assassination of Ronald Reagan">attempted assassination</a> of US President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, first lady <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Reagan" title="Nancy Reagan">Nancy Reagan</a> commissioned astrologer <a href="/wiki/Joan_Quigley" title="Joan Quigley">Joan Quigley</a> to act as the secret White House astrologer. However, Quigley's role ended in 1988 when it became public through the memoirs of former chief of staff, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Regan" title="Donald Regan">Donald Regan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was a boom in interest in astrology in the late 1960s. The sociologist <a href="/wiki/Marcello_Truzzi" title="Marcello Truzzi">Marcello Truzzi</a> described three levels of involvement of "Astrology-believers" to account for its revived popularity in the face of scientific discrediting. He found that most astrology-believers did not claim it was a scientific explanation with predictive power. Instead, those superficially involved, knowing "next to nothing" about astrology's 'mechanics', read newspaper astrology columns, and could benefit from "tension-management of anxieties" and "a cognitive belief-system that transcends science."<sup id="cite_ref-Truzzi_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Truzzi-141">[140]</a></sup> Those at the second level usually had their horoscopes cast and sought advice and predictions. They were much younger than those at the first level, and could benefit from knowledge of the language of astrology and the resulting ability to belong to a coherent and exclusive group. Those at the third level were highly involved and usually cast horoscopes for themselves. Astrology provided this small minority of astrology-believers with a "<i>meaningful</i> view of their universe and [gave] them an <i>understanding</i> of their place in it."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[b]</a></sup> This third group took astrology seriously, possibly as a <i>sacred canopy</i>, whereas the other two groups took it playfully and irreverently.<sup id="cite_ref-Truzzi_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Truzzi-141">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1953, sociologist <a href="/wiki/Theodor_W._Adorno" title="Theodor W. Adorno">Theodor W. Adorno</a> conducted a study of the astrology column of a Los Angeles newspaper as part of a project examining mass culture in capitalist society.<sup id="cite_ref-Nederman_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nederman-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:326</sup> Adorno believed that popular astrology, as a device, invariably leads to statements that encouraged conformityâ€”and that astrologers who go against conformity, by discouraging performance at work etc., risk losing their jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-Nederman_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nederman-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:327</sup> Adorno concluded that astrology was a large-scale manifestation of systematic <a href="/wiki/Irrationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irrationalism">irrationalism</a>, where individuals are subtly ledâ€”through flattery and vague generalisationsâ€”to believe that the author of the column is addressing them directly.<sup id="cite_ref-Adorno_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adorno-144">[142]</a></sup> Adorno drew a parallel with the phrase <a href="/wiki/Opium_of_the_people" title="Opium of the people">opium of the people</a>, by Karl Marx, by commenting, "occultism is the metaphysic of the dopes."<sup id="cite_ref-Nederman_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nederman-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:329</sup></p>
<p>A 2005 <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> poll and a 2009 survey by the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> reported that 25% of US adults believe in astrology.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup> According to data released in the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation's</a> 2014 <i>Science and Engineering Indicators</i> study, "Fewer Americans rejected astrology in 2012 than in recent years."<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-147">[145]</a></sup> The NSF study noted that in 2012, "slightly more than half of Americans said that astrology was 'not at all scientific,' whereas nearly two-thirds gave this response in 2010. The comparable percentage has not been this low since 1983."<sup id="cite_ref-NSF_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-147">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India_and_Japan">India and Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Astrology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: India and Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg/220px-Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg/330px-Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg/440px-Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="320" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bdrates_of_Japan_since_1950.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Birth (in blue) and death (in red) rates of Japan since 1950, with the sudden drop in births during hinoeuma year (1966)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In India, there is a long-established and widespread belief in astrology. It is commonly used for daily life, particularly in matters concerning marriage and career, and makes extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Electional_astrology" title="Electional astrology">electional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horary_astrology" title="Horary astrology">horary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karmic_astrology" title="Karmic astrology">karmic astrology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wideind_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wideind-148">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fof_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fof-149">[147]</a></sup> Indian politics have also been influenced by astrology.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> It is still considered a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Vedanga" title="Vedanga">Vedanga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> In 2001, Indian scientists and politicians debated and critiqued a proposal to use state money to fund research into astrology,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_India_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_India-153">[151]</a></sup> resulting in permission for <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_India" title="List of universities in India">Indian universities</a> to offer courses in Vedic astrology.<sup id="cite_ref-UGC_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UGC-154">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Bombay_High_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombay High Court">Bombay High Court</a> reaffirmed astrology's standing in India when it dismissed a case that challenged its status as a science.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, strong belief in astrology has led to dramatic changes in the fertility rate and the number of abortions in the years of <i>Fire Horse</i>. Adherents believe that women born in <i>hinoeuma</i> years are unmarriageable and bring bad luck to their father or husband. In 1966, the number of babies born in Japan dropped by over 25% as parents tried to avoid the stigma of having a daughter born in the hinoeuma year.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_music">Literature and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Astrology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Literature and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman_in_the_Moon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Woman_in_the_Moon.jpg/170px-Woman_in_the_Moon.jpg" width="170" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Woman_in_the_Moon.jpg/255px-Woman_in_the_Moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Woman_in_the_Moon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="284" data-file-height="456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman_in_the_Moon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page of <a href="/wiki/John_Lyly" title="John Lyly">John Lyly's</a> astrological play, <i>The Woman in the Moon</i>, 1597</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The fourteenth-century English poets <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">John Gower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> both referred to astrology in their works, including Gower's <i><a href="/wiki/Confessio_Amantis" title="Confessio Amantis">Confessio Amantis</a></i> and Chaucer's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">The Canterbury Tales</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wedel_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wedel-158">[156]</a></sup> Chaucer commented explicitly on astrology in his <i>Treatise on the Astrolabe</i>, demonstrating personal knowledge of one area, judicial astrology, with an account of how to find the ascendant or rising sign.<sup id="cite_ref-WoodAstrolabe_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WoodAstrolabe-159">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the fifteenth century, references to astrology, such as with <a href="/wiki/Simile" title="Simile">similes</a>, became "a matter of course" in English literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Wedel_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wedel-158">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the sixteenth century, John Lyly's 1597 play, <i>The Woman in the Moon</i>, is wholly motivated by astrology,<sup id="cite_ref-DeLacy_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeLacy-160">[158]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a> makes astrological references in his plays <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Faustus_(play)" title="Doctor Faustus (play)">Doctor Faustus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tamburlaine" title="Tamburlaine">Tamburlaine</a></i> (both c. 1590),<sup id="cite_ref-DeLacy_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeLacy-160">[158]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sir_Philip_Sidney" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Philip Sidney">Sir Philip Sidney</a> refers to astrology at least four times in his <a href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance (heroic literature)">romance</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Countess_of_Pembroke%27s_Arcadia" title="The Countess of Pembroke's Arcadia">The Countess of Pembroke's Arcadia</a></i> (c. 1580).<sup id="cite_ref-DeLacy_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeLacy-160">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a> uses astrology both decoratively and causally in his poetry, revealing "...unmistakably an abiding interest in the art, an interest shared by a large number of his contemporaries."<sup id="cite_ref-DeLacy_160-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeLacy-160">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Chapman" title="George Chapman">George Chapman's</a> play, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Conspiracy_and_Tragedy_of_Charles,_Duke_of_Byron" title="The Conspiracy and Tragedy of Charles, Duke of Byron">Byron's Conspiracy</a></i> (1608), similarly uses astrology as a causal mechanism in the drama.<sup id="cite_ref-Camden_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Camden-161">[159]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare's</a> attitude towards astrology is unclear, with contradictory references in plays including <i><a href="/wiki/King_Lear" title="King Lear">King Lear</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Antony_and_Cleopatra" title="Antony and Cleopatra">Antony and Cleopatra</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_II_(play)" title="Richard II (play)">Richard II</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Camden_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Camden-161">[159]</a></sup> Shakespeare was familiar with astrology and made use of his knowledge of astrology in nearly every play he wrote,<sup id="cite_ref-Camden_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Camden-161">[159]</a></sup> assuming a basic familiarity with the subject in his commercial audience.<sup id="cite_ref-Camden_161-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Camden-161">[159]</a></sup> Outside theatre, the physician and mystic <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fludd" title="Robert Fludd">Robert Fludd</a> practised astrology, as did the quack doctor Simon Forman.<sup id="cite_ref-Camden_161-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Camden-161">[159]</a></sup> In Elizabethan England, "The usual feeling about astrology&#160;... [was] that it is the most useful of the sciences."<sup id="cite_ref-Camden_161-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Camden-161">[159]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg/170px-Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg/255px-Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg/340px-Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="825" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astrologo_Fingido_Calderon_de_la_Barca_title_page_1641.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page of <a href="/wiki/Calder%C3%B3n_de_la_Barca" class="mw-redirect" title="CalderÃ³n de la Barca">CalderÃ³n de la Barca's</a> <i>Astrologo Fingido</i>, Madrid, 1641</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In seventeenth century Spain, <a href="/wiki/Lope_de_Vega" title="Lope de Vega">Lope de Vega</a>, with a detailed knowledge of astronomy, wrote plays that ridicule astrology. In his pastoral romance <i>La Arcadia</i> (1598), it leads to absurdity; in his novela <i>Guzman el Bravo</i> (1624), he concludes that the stars were made for man, not man for the stars.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calder%C3%B3n_de_la_Barca" class="mw-redirect" title="CalderÃ³n de la Barca">CalderÃ³n de la Barca</a> wrote the 1641 comedy <i>Astrologo Fingido</i> (The Pretended Astrologer); the plot was borrowed by the French playwright <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Corneille" title="Thomas Corneille">Thomas Corneille</a> for his 1651 comedy <i>Feint Astrologue</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most famous piece of music influenced by astrology is the orchestral suite <i><a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">The Planets</a></i>. Written by the British composer <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Holst" title="Gustav Holst">Gustav Holst</a> (1874â€“1934), and first performed in 1918, the framework of <i>The Planets</i> is based upon the astrological symbolism of the planets.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> Each of the seven movements of the suite is based upon a different planet, though the movements are not in the order of the planets from the Sun. The composer <a href="/wiki/Colin_Matthews" title="Colin Matthews">Colin Matthews</a> wrote an eighth movement entitled <i>Pluto, the Renewer</i>, first performed in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> In 1937, another British composer, <a href="/wiki/Constant_Lambert" title="Constant Lambert">Constant Lambert</a>, wrote a ballet on astrological themes, called <i><a href="/wiki/Horoscope_(ballet)" title="Horoscope (ballet)">Horoscope</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup> In 1974, the New Zealand composer <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Carr_(composer)" title="Edwin Carr (composer)">Edwin Carr</a> wrote <i>The Twelve Signs: An Astrological Entertainment</i> for orchestra without strings.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Camille_Paglia" title="Camille Paglia">Camille Paglia</a> acknowledges astrology as an influence on her work of literary criticism <i><a href="/wiki/Sexual_Personae" title="Sexual Personae">Sexual Personae</a></i> (1990).<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Astrology features strongly in <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Catton" title="Eleanor Catton">Eleanor Catton's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Luminaries" title="The Luminaries">The Luminaries</a></i>, recipient of the <a href="/wiki/2013_Man_Booker_Prize" title="2013 Man Booker Prize">2013 Man Booker Prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup></p>

<ul>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nsf.gov/statistics/seind06/c7/c7s2.htm#c7s2l3">"Chapter 7: Science and Technology: Public Attitudes and Understanding"</a>. <i>science and engineering indicators 2006</i>. </ul>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Hartmann-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Hartmann_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hartmann_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Hartmann, P.; Reuter, M.; Nyborga, H. "The relationship between date of birth and individual differences in personality and general intelligence: A large-scale study". <i>Personality and Individual Differences</i> <b>40</b> (7): 1349â€“1362. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.paid.2005.11.017">10.1016/j.paid.2005.11.017</a>. <q>To optimise the chances of finding even remote relationships between date of birth and individual differences in personality and intelligence we further applied two different strategies. <li id="cite_note-Durling-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Durling_57-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Durling_57-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Durling, Robert M. In 1940 Rudolf Palgen published his pioneering eighty-page Dantes Sternglaube: BeitrÃ¤ge zur ErklÃ¤rung des Paradiso, which concisely surveyed Dante's treatment of the planets and of the sphere of fixed stars; he demonstrated that it is governed by the astrological concept of the "children of the planets" (in each sphere the pilgrim meets souls whose lives reflected the dominant influence of that planet) and that in countless details the imagery of the Paradiso is derived from the astrological tradition.&#160;... <li id="cite_note-Allum-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Allum_114-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Allum, Nick (13 December 2010). "What Makes Some People Think Astrology Is Scientific?". <i>Science Communication</i> <b>33</b> (3): 341â€“366. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1177%2F1075547010389819">10.1177/1075547010389819</a>. <q>This underlies the <i>Barnum effect</i>. Named after the 19th-century showman Phileas T. Barnumâ€”whose circus provided "a little something for everyone"â€”it refers to the idea that people believe a statement about their personality that is vague or trivial if they think it derives from some systematic procedure tailored especially for them (Dickson &amp; Kelly, 1985; Furnham &amp; Schofield, 1987; Rogers &amp; Soule, 2009; Wyman &amp; Vyse, 2008). For example, the more birth detail is used in an astrological prediction or horoscope, the more credulous people tend to be (Furnham, 1991). However, confirmation bias means that people do not tend to pay attention to other information that might disconfirm the credibility of the predictions.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAstrology&amp;rft.atitle=What+Makes+Some+People+Think+Astrology+Is+Scientific%3F&amp;rft.aufirst=Nick&amp;rft.aulast=Allum&amp;rft.date=2010-12-13&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1177%2F1075547010389819&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Science+Communication&amp;rft.pages=341-366&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=33" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Wedel-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Wedel_158-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wedel_158-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Wedel, Theodore Otto (2003) [1920]. "9: Astrology in Gower and Chaucer". <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=V9lVQVyb9M4C&amp;pg=PA133&amp;lpg=PA133&amp;dq=%22Confessio+Amantis%22+Gower+astrology#v=onepage&amp;q=%22Confessio%20Amantis%22%20Gower%20astrology&amp;f=false"><i>MediÃ¦val Attitude Toward Astrology, Particularly in England</i></a>. Kessinger. pp.&#160;131â€“156. <q>The literary interest in astrology, which had been on the increase in England throughout the fourteenth century, culminated in the works of Gower and Chaucer. Although references to astrology were already frequent in the romances of the fourteenth century, these still retained the signs of being foreign importations. It was only in the fifteenth century that astrological similes and embellishments became a matter of course in the literature of England.<br />
Such innovations, one must confess, were due far more to Chaucer than to Gower. Gower, too, saw artistic possibilities in the new astrological learning, and promptly used these in his retelling of the Alexander legendâ€”but he confined himself, for the most part, to a bald rehearsal of facts and theories. It is, accordingly, as a part of the long encyclopaedia of natural science that he inserted into his <i>Confessio Amantis</i>, and in certain didactic passages of the <i>Vox Clamantis</i> and the <i>Mirour de l'Omme</i>, that Astrology figures most largely in his works&#160;... Gower's sources on the subject of astrology&#160;... were Albumasar's <i>Introductorium in Astronomiam</i>, the Pseudo-Aristotelian <i>Secretum Secretorum</i>, Brunetto Latini's <i>TrÃ©sor</i>, and the <i>Speculum Astronomiae</i> ascribed to Albert the Great.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAstrology&amp;rft.atitle=9%3A+Astrology+in+Gower+and+Chaucer&amp;rft.au=Wedel%2C+Theodore+Otto&amp;rft.btitle=Medi%C3%A6val+Attitude+Toward+Astrology%2C+Particularly+in+England&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DV9lVQVyb9M4C%26pg%3DPA133%26lpg%3DPA133%26dq%3D%2522Confessio%2BAmantis%2522%2BGower%2Bastrology%23v%3Donepage%26q%3D%2522Confessio%2520Amantis%2522%2520Gower%2520astrology%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.pages=131-156&amp;rft.pub=Kessinger&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-WoodAstrolabe-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-WoodAstrolabe_159-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wood, 1970. pp.12â€“21</span></li>

University of Oklahoma Press.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAstrology&amp;rft.au=Osborn%2C+M.&amp;rft.btitle=Time+and+the+Astrolabe+in+The+Canterbury+Tales&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%2Fabout%2FTime_and_the_astrolabe_in_the_Canterbury.html%3Fid%3Dtwt6-FgU-EkC&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Oklahoma+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>

"The True Place of Astrology in the History of Science". <i>Isis</i> <b>46</b> (3): 273. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1086%2F348412">10.1086/348412</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAstrology&amp;rft.atitle=The+True+Place+of+Astrology+in+the+History+of+Science&amp;rft.au=Thorndike%2C+Lynn&amp;rft.date=1955&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1086%2F348412&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Isis&amp;rft.pages=273&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=46" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>
